POONA:

*Printed at the Ambaprasad Press, Civil Line,*

———————  
1897.

**DEDICATED**

**TO MY FATHER**

IN TOKEN OF HIS WONDERFUL FATHERLY SELF-SACRIFICE

II ENABLING ME TO ENJOY THE FRUITS OF HIGHER EDUCATION.

HIS DUTIFUL SON

**The Editor.**

**PREFACE.**  
**————————**

 The work of editing this part of the poem prescribed by the University of Bombay for the Students of the Previous Examination of this year was undertaken as early as the month of January, 1896. But the publication had to be delayed owing to pecuniary difficulties which did not allow the editor to do it upon his own responsibility. The editor had almost given up intention of giving light to these notes but some friends of his who were requested to pronounce their judgment on the merits of the work, thought it highly adapted to meet the requirements of the students for whom this humble attempt is made, and encouraged him to publish it and by a happy coincidence the manager of Shiralkar Company in Poona enpressed his ready willingness to take up in his hands the publication of the work. The birth of the book is therefore entirely due to his kindness for which the editor is greatly indebted to him.

 The editor frankly admits that he has no pretensions to the knowledge of Sanskrit Grammar, through all its labyrinth of intricacies and nicity of peculiarities, beyond what he had gained by his study of ordinary grammars on the subject. He has no occasion to go to the original works such as Panini's Ashtádháyi or Battoji Dikshit's Koumudi. But he ventures to remark that in the elucidation of the text he has not failed to explain grammatical forms &c. The student will find almost all his difficulties, either grammatical, allusive, or critical solved in the notes. The editor thinks it polite not to speak at length on the merits of the work which is his first attempt to render humble service to the cause of Sanskrit litera-

ture which being national deserves as much study at least as we bestow upon foreign literature. This he leaves to the critic with one remark that he has spared no pains to make the book as serviceable to the student class for whom it is designed, as he can do with all his best efforts.

 One more remark before he closes the preface. The Batti Kávya, no doubt, is mainly intended to furnish a tolerably complete knowledge of Sanskrit Grammar but in the case of a student who has mastered as much grammar as he is reasonably expected to do for his matriculation this cannot be a main object. A more detailed knowledge of Grammar in all its various branches, etymology, philology &c. is, the editor ventures to think, the work and aim of the specialist but what is expected, even by the University is not the knowledge of the words so much which form only the body, as it were, in which the spirit of the poet is clothed, as the deep insight into the poetry of the book. A beginning is to be made in the first year of collegiate education to accustom oneself to read the mind of the poet which will gradually furnish the student with that critical insight and poetic depth which is the first mark of highly cultured minds. To forget oneself for the time being and become one with the poet's mind is a great educator of the feelings which no amount of word knowledge can educate.

 The editor takes this opportunity to express his heart-felt thanks to all those who have in one way or another rendered him valuable assistance,

 A few errors have crept in but they are purely those of the printing and not therefore embodied in an errata.

Panvel,  
31st March 1897                            

V. G. PRADHAN.

———————————————

"क्लेशः फलेनहि पुनर्नवतां विधत्ते"

INTRODUCTION  
I  
On the Date of the Poet.  
≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈

 The most difficult task in the field of Sanskrit Literature is the assignation of the date of the person or event about whom or which one has occasion to write. One is beset with innumerable difficulties when one undertakes such an enquiry; from the fact that there is almost a universal absence of direct evidence as such from which any reasonable conclusion can be drawn. The Indian mind is peculiarly noted for its utter repugnance to such subjects which appear as of little or no value to it. Even in this nineteenth century we have instances of men of the old type to whom these questions appear very insignificant and they look with an indifferent eye upon every attempt that is made to settle the question of the date of a particular event or person noted in ancient Indian History. To such persons all these efforts are so much waste of time and energy. What wonder, then, that no attempt worth the name was made by the ancient Hindus to write on such points. They cherished the utmost sense of respect for the men, events, &c., of their ancient national history; but the exact time of their occurrence seemed, to them, purely worthless. Even in our historical works we do not find that exactness and strictness of the historian who mentions, with utmost caution, hypothetical conjectures or imaginary events. "In studying. the history of the earliest civilized nations of the world, we are unable to fix dates, or to trace the course of events with the degree of accuracy which marks modern history..."......" It is still more so in respect of India "." They tell us very little of kings and dynasties; and even when such lists are available, they are bare lists of names, and have little value in a true historical sense[^1]". It is, therefore, why so much discussion, argument, and contention have to be employed in founding an Indian chronology. There are, no doubt, ample materials which can be gathered, shifted, and manipulated; but the work entails an infinite amount of labour and requires dogged perseverance, steady research, and patient study from this very reason that the materials are as vague as possible in this respect. For one hypothesis we have to depend upon an equally doubtful hypothesis. All that we can do, is to fix the priority of one person over another-that too with

[^1]: # "Ancient India, by R. C. Dutt, p. 3."

great reservation—but no exact date is possible. If no contemporary writings either of the Greek or Chinese authors had existed the work of constructing Indian chronology would have been an utterly impossible task. But thanks to these writings we are now in a tolerably secure position. Their aid is simply invaluable.

 These remarks will give the reader an idea of the immense difficulty which surrounds our enquiry. We shall, therefore, have to take in the aid of internal and external evidence to establish the question of the date of our poet. By internal evidence, we mean any thing that is contained in the poem which will enable us to find *raison d'etre* for arriving at a conclusion on this point. By external evidence we mean writings above referred to. The antiquarian researches of the Oriental Scholars, native as well as foreign based upon these writings, will help us much in our investigation.

 Some of the Sanskrit writers allude to their descent, patrons, &c. in their writings; but they are, strange to say, deficient in the very thing we want. They do so much but omit to mention the time which they could have done with equal easiness. But time was no question with them; enough that they had done to extol their family or praise their patron: Our poet thus writes at the end of his poem :—

**काव्यमिदं विहितं मया वलभ्यां श्रीधरसेननरेन्द्रपालितायाम्।  
कीर्ति रतो भवतान्नृपस्य तस्यप्रेमकरः क्षितिपो यतः प्रजानाम्॥  
                                 Can. XXII. 35.**

 The poet makes mention of the place and the king-probably his own patron—where and during whose regime he composed his poem. This declaration of the poet at the end gives an important clue to his time, thus bringing the question within the pale of historic investigation. The only thing the historian has to do, is to determine who this king Shridharasena was; the time when the monarch flourished. We shall have to determine the par ticular king bearing the name, intended by the poet as ruling in this time; for we learn from the ancient history of the Sourástra (the modern peninsula of Kattiawar) that there were three kings who were similarly named and who ruled the country in succession. It appears from the history of this country that the old Dwàrkà called Avartanagari or Abdhinagari was the first metropolis and Valabhi received that honor later on about the fourth century of the Christian era. According to Cunningham 'the old name of Souràstra was lost in A. D. 319, when the successors of the Sàh kings were supplanted by the Vallabhas, and the capital changed from Junagurh to Valabhi. The establishment of the Balabhi era, which dates from A. D. 319, is said by Abu Rihan to mark the period of the extinction of the Gupta race, whose coins are found in considerable numbers in Gujarát. This

date may therefore be accepted with some certainty as that of the establishment of the Balabhi dynasty, and most probably also as that of the foundation of the city of Balabhi[^2]." Though we may accept the year 319 A. D. as that of the establishment of the Balabhi dynasty we shall be wrong to suppose that this dynasty was Independent. It was at that time a tributary of the Gupta race of kings who were in those days the emperors of Northern India. After the death of Skandha Gupta, the last powerful potentate of the Gupta dynasty, Balabhi acquired independence under Bhattárka. "The Vallabhis had founded an independent kingdom in Gujarath under Bhatárka about 460 A. D. when the power of the Guptas of Kanoja was on the decline, and the dynasty of Bhatarka ruled Gujarat for three hundred years[^3]..."

[^2]: # "Ancient Geography of India, vol- I p.318"

[^3]: # "Ancient India, R. C. Dutt, p. 149"

 The king to whom allusion is made by our poet, appears to have been an immediate successor of Bhatarka; though it must be borne in mind that we do not possess any detailed history of this period of this country, which will, in any way, enable us to come to a definite conclusion as regards the personality of the king referred to by our poet. That there were three kings of the name of Shridharasena of the Vala bhi dynasty we know for certain from copper inscriptions which furnish us with a geneological table of this kingly family. Prof. Lassen supposes that a king of this name of the Valabhi dynasty, reigned from 530 to 545 A. D. If now we take it for granted that this might be the last king of that name, we shall have to admit the position that our poet lived about the end of the first half of the six century, A. D. This then will be the terminius ad queno of the date of our poet, But it will not be historically incorrect to place our poet at least a century earlier than the period proposed by Prof. Lassen, if not still earlier. For, this supposition is warrantable on the ground that we can reasonably allow a century to have elapsed between the first king of the name of Shridharasena and the last king of that name on the theory of their unbroken succession, which is a great concession; since the geneological table above spoken about, places, at least, three kings between first Shridharasena and the second and many to intervene between the second and the third. inclined to think that the king Shridharasena whom Prof. Lassen places between 530-545 A.D.might be the second king of that name. Now the first Shridharasena immediately succeeds Bhattàrka and can therefore be reasonably said to have reigned in the end of the fourth century or in the very beginning of the fifth century. Moreover in the sacred *kalpasutra* of the Jainas it is written that in the year 992 after the death or Nirvana of Swami Mabâvîra,

i\. e. in 465 A. D. Druvasena, the successor of Shridharasena held a public recital of that sacred book. Again, though above we have accepted the year 460 A. D. as the year of the establishment of the Valabhi dynasty by Bhattarka on the authority of the author quoted there, the authority cannot be unquestionable; for as we have seen above, Cunningham assings a different date to this event and we find the Shaka year 310 given as the year[^4]. From all these data we are strongly disposed to conclude that our poet may be historically placed in the end of the fourth century, A. D.; at least this can be the *terminus a quo* of his date.

[^4]: # "Sursotra Desâcha sasaksha Itihasa, by Lokahitvadi, p. 22,"

 The style of a poet is looked upon as one of the internal evidences. In the history of Sanskrit Literature this is more so than in any other. It cannot be disputed that the earlier the poot, the easier his diction. This is absolutely true in the case of Kálidása whose poetic style is materially different from that of the poets who have followed him in the field of Sanskrit Literature. A comparison of Kálidása 's style with that of Bhavabhuti or Bána, will at once make this point very clear. Now we find upon our perusal of the Bhattikàvya that its style is as simple, even despite its being a grammatical poem, as that of Kálidása . This is true even in the case of the cantos we have to study. The peculiar characteristics of this kind of style, are that the diction is simple, almost free from those long compounds which so much distinguish the writers of a subsequent period, less figurative, with less metrical embellishment. All these distinguishing features of this mode of poetic language are present in the poem of our author, Kálidása and Bhatti, therefore, belong in our opinion, to the same age, nay it is possible to suppose that Bhatti is prior to Kálidása since the diction of the latter is comparatively more figurative &c. This is a safe conclusion on the authority of those oriental scholars who assign the six century after Christ to Kálidása; for Kálidása, according to them, was a poet of the court of Vikramaditya who is placed, by these same scholars in the sixth century after Christ. But we must confess that we are unprepared, for want of solid and convincing arguments to accept their verdict. Our opinion is that Kálidása decidedly belongs to an age before Christ. This helps us to put our author before the Christian era. We must, however, admit that we have no sufficient materials authorising us to assign some period before Christ to our poet; though we are pretty fully convinced that we cannot separate our poet from Kálidása by a great distance of time.

II  
On the Identity of Bhatti with Bhartṛihari.

 This is a question which claims our attention. Tradition lends support to the view that Bhatti is the same as Bhartrihari;

but the question is not easy of solution. Even there is a controversy as regards the identity of the Bhartrihari who is the author of the Satakas with that Bhartrihari who is the recognised author of the Vakyapadiya. The late Mr. Justice Telang says about this question: "We have already by implication answered this question in the affirmative on the authority of tradition. That tradition is alluded to by Colebrook who does not question it, and the same remark applies to Bohlen." But we know that we require more than mere authority of tradition to accept his position. However we shall speak of this later on.

 As regards the question under our consideration. That there was a tradition, as mentioned above, which identified Bhartrihari with Bhatti is clear enough. Thus in the commentary of Jayamangala on *Bhattikavya* the name of the author is given as Bhatti and he is described as "Sri Swamisunu" and as a great grammarian— श्रीस्वामिसूनुः कविर्भट्टिनामा महावैयाकरणो रामकथाश्रय महाकाव्यं चकार—. Again in the commentary of Bharata on Bhatti, the name of the author is given as Bhartrihari but nothing more is said about him. On this point Mr. Telang thus pronounces himself: "For an affirmative answer to this question, there is no better or stronger authority than the identity of name but even this ground is exceedingly weak." Farther on, after quoting a good many Oriental scholars, he declares: "From this conspectus of opinions the conclusions deducible seem to be, that the identity of the too Bhartriharis (i. e. Bhartrihari and Ehatti) rests on the frailest possible foundation, and that it is only just possible that there may have been some connection between them, but which of those alleged has the best voucher it is difficult to say."

 We, fer our own part, are disposed to consider the author of the Bhattikavya as not identical with the Bhartrihari who is the reputed author of the *Vakyapadiya* for the following reasons. Mr. K. B. Pathak, in his essay on "Was Bhartrihari a Buddhist?" has conclusively shown that the author of the Vakyapadiya was a Buddhist on the authority of the Chinese traveller I-tsing who tells us that the grammarian who wrote the Vakyapadıya was a Buddhist, that he believed deeply in the three jewels aud meditated on the two-fold voidness. Having desired (to embrace) the excellent religion, he belonged to the priestly order, but overcome by wordly desires, he returned to the laity." Another ground for

but the question is not easy of solution. Even there is a controversy as regards the identity of the Bhartrihari who is the author of the Satakas with that Bhartrihari who is the recognised author of the Vakyapadiya. The late Mr. Justice Telang says about this question: "We have already by implication answered this question in the affirmative on the authority of tradition. That tradition is alluded to by Colebrook who does not question it, and the same remark applies to Bohlen." But we know that we require more than mere authority of tradition to accept his position. However we shall speak of this later on.

 As regards the question under our consideration. That there was a tradition, as mentioned above, which identified Bhartrihari with Bhatti is clear enough. Thus in the commentary of Jayamangala on *Bhattikavya* the name of the author is given as Bhatti and he is described as "Sri Swamisunu" and as a great grammarian— श्रीस्वामिसूनुः कविर्भट्टिनामा महावैयाकरणो रामकथाश्रय महाकाव्यं चकार—. Again in the commentary of Bharata on Bhatti, the name of the author is given as Bhartrihari but nothing more is said about him. On this point Mr. Telang thus pronounces himself: "For an affirmative answer to this question, there is no better or stronger authority than the identity of name but even this ground is exceedingly weak." Farther on, after quoting a good many Oriental scholars, he declares: "From this conspectus of opinions the conclusions deducible seem to be, that the identity of the too Bhartriharis (i. e. Bhartrihari and Ehatti) rests on the frailest possible foundation, and that it is only just possible that there may have been some connection between them, but which of those alleged has the best voucher it is difficult to say."

 We, fer our own part, are disposed to consider the author of the Bhattikavya as not identical with the Bhartrihari who is the reputed author of the *Vakyapadiya* for the following reasons. Mr. K. B. Pathak, in his essay on "Was Bhartrihari a Buddhist?" has conclusively shown that the author of the Vakyapadiya was a Buddhist on the authority of the Chinese traveller I-tsing who tells us that the grammarian who wrote the Vakyapadıya was a Buddhist, that he believed deeply in the three jewels and meditated on the two-fold voidness. Having desired (to embrace) the excellent religion, he belonged to the priestly order, but overcome by worldly desires, he returned to the laity." Another ground for

this conclusion is the assertion of Vàchaspatimisra who speaks of the author of the Vakyapadiya as one of the Bahyas[^5], a term that is derisively applied to Buddhistic writers. Telang, no doubt, rejects the authority of I-tsing; but we agree with Mr. Pathak when he says: "The one great principle which guides or ought to guide historical investigations is that we should accept without reserve contemporary evidence about past events, whenever such evidence is available to our inquiries[^6]." Thus the question of the identity of Bhatti with this Bhartrihari is negatively proved. Now as regards the identity of the Bhartrihari of the Sâtakas with our poet we may allow this presumption if we think that there is no Buddhistic flavour about the Satakas. This is still an open question.

[^5]: # "Ibid, p. 3."

[^6]: # "Ibid, p. 6."

III.  
On the Merits of the Poem.

 In this last section of the Introduction we propose to express our views on the merits of the poem. The *Bhattikavya* is, as we know, honoured with the high name of a Mahakavya, i. e. a great classical poem. It is therefore essential to examine the grounds, both technical and general, upon which the claim of this poem to the high honour of a Mahakavya is established. By technical grounds we mean the rules of Sanskrit rhetoricians laid down in their books on Poetics. By general grounds we mean how far the poem is good poetry tested by the requisites of true poetry—how much there is of the poetic mind. We think that the general grounds deserve more attention than technical rules which must have followed the study of the former and which in their hard-and-fast nature are trammels which have the pernicious effects of taking out the poet from his poetry. No true poet cares to be led by the nose by the rules of his critics. His poetic nature bursts from the fetters which bind it and revels in its own soarings.

 We know that the Bhattikavya is a grammatical poem, i. e. a poem which has for its chief aim, the object of aiding the student in the study of grammar of the Sanskrit Language. The task the poet proposed for himself was thus made doubly difficult. He must not fail to do justice to his foremost object. Nor must he altogether sacrifice poetry to this object. It would not do for him to create poetry with no spirit in it because he meant to teach grammar. He must put in something of the poet in his grammar because it is this form which he chooses as the medium of grammatical instruction. But we shall soon find

that our poet was fully alive to this. He possessed decidedly powers of the poet which helped him to perform his task satisfactorily in both the respects. He has shown himself grammatical and poetic at the same time. About the grammatical nature of his poem we need not speak at length. That it is a great help in that respect is admitted on all hands. He has been approvingly quoted by great grammarians like Bhattoji Dikshit. We therefore proceed to speak of his poetry. First the technical grounds:

 It is now necessary to give the definition of a Mahakavya. The Kavyadarsa thus defines it:

**सर्गबन्धो महाकाव्यमुच्यते तस्य लक्षणम्। आशीर्नमस्क्रियावस्तुनिर्द्देशो वापितन्मुखम्॥  
इतिहासकथोद्भूत भितरद्वा सदाश्रयम्। चतुर्वर्गफलोपेतं चतुरोदात्तनायकम्॥१५॥**

**नगरार्णवशैलतेचन्द्रार्कोदयवर्णनैः। उद्यानसलिलक्रिडामधुपानरतोत्सवैः॥१६॥**

**विप्रलम्भैर्विवाहेश्च कुमारोदयवर्णनैः। मन्त्रदूतप्रयाणानिनायकाभ्युदयैरपि॥१७॥**

**अलङ्कृतमसङ्क्षिप्तं रसभावनिरन्तरम्। सर्गैरनतिविस्तीर्णैः श्रव्यवृत्तैः सुसन्धिभिः॥१८॥**

**सर्वत्रभिन्नवृत्तान्तैरुपेतं लोकरञ्जकम्। काव्यं कल्पान्तरस्थायिजायेन सदलङ्कृति॥१९॥**

 The student will find these conditions of a Mahakavya prescribed by writers on poetics are fulfilled in our poem. We need not dwell upon them in this small Introduction. On technical grounds, therefore, our poet can reasonably claim the appellation of a great classical poem' to his own poem. In the Notes, our note on Bhattikavya gives full information about the nature of a kavya and on many other points which form the essentials of a Mahakavya. We now come to those merits which belong essentially to the nature of the poet, A classical poem must have the charms of true poetry. It must give highest pleasure to its appreciative reader by its rhythmical melody and sweetness of language, by its poetic reflections, and richness of poetic imagery, by the flights of poetic fancy and its communion with the beauties of nature. The sublime and the beautiful must find a prominent place in poetry. It must appeal to the heart more than to the head and make the reader's mind for the time being, one with that of the poet. The reader must be led to weep with the poet, to rejoice with his rejoicing, to think with his thinking and so on. The poet in short must possess a richly capacious mind, a fertile imagination, a ever flowing fancy, and a faculty of observation ever open to impression, which make him the master of his art. With these general remarks upon what the poem and poetry must be we come to the particular sphere of our critical examination, i. e. the merits of our poet.

 In the Notes we have not missed the opportunity of drawing the reader's attention to the beauties of our poem. We will, therefore, in this place, only put them together and conclude. The student will notice considerable rhythmical melody in the poem. In spite of the grammatical form, the poetry runs smooth and harmonious. From the opening description of the second Canto (videparticularly St. 4, 6,7 9, 12, 13, 15, 16, 18, 19 Can. II) and numerous descriptions elsewhere, all pointed out in the Notes in their places, we feel bound to say that our poet has much of the poetic nature in him. His imagination is fertile enough, his fancy is at its work, his observation of natural scenery is clear enough. Bhatti is, therefore, a poet of considerable merit giving clear evidences of poetic mind of no mean order. We cannot rank him with Kálidása or Bhavabhuti but it is equally true that we cannot dethrone him from the high pedestal of poetical name and honour. He is essentially a poet of the second magnitude. But he gives clear signs of poetical originality and dignity. He has honoured the Muse, not  
degraded her.

 In conclusion we request the indulgence of our critics towards this first humble effort to do some service to the cause of Sanskrit Literature.

**\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_ \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_**

**॥श्री॥**

**भट्टिकाव्यम्।  
≈≈≈≈≈≈**

**प्रथमः सर्गः।  
≈≈≈≈≈≈**

**अभुन्नृपो विबुधसखः परन्तपः श्रुतान्वितो दशरथ इत्युदाहृतः।  
गुणैर्वरं भुवनहितच्छलेन यं सनातनः पितरमुपागमत्स्वयम्॥१॥**

**सोऽध्यैष्ट वेदांस्त्रिदशानयष्ट पितॄनपारीत्सममंस्त बन्धून्।  
व्यजेष्ट षड्वर्गपरंस्त नीतौ समूलघातं न्यवधीदर्रीश्च॥२॥**

**वसूनि तोयं घनत्रद्वयकारीत्सहासनं गोत्रभिदाध्यवात्सीत्।  
न त्र्यम्बकादन्यमुपास्थितासौ यशांसि सर्वेषुभृतां निरास्थत्॥३॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 1. (Anciently) there flourished a king by name Daśaratha, the friend of Indra, a chastiser of his enemies, well versed in the Vedic literature; to him eminent by his virtues, was born the Primeval Being voluntarily for the good of the worlds.

 2. He studied the Vedas, sacrificed to the gods, fed his manes, showed regard to his relations, conquered the six passions, took delight in right conduct, and completely destroyed his enemies.

 3. He distributed wealth (among his people) as the cloud showers water, he occupied a seat with Indra, did not worship any other god than S'iva, and he obscured the fame of all archers.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**पुण्यो महाब्रह्मसमूहजुष्टः सन्तर्पणो नाकसदां वरेण्यः।  
जज्वाल लोकस्थितये स राजा यथाध्वरे वह्निरभिप्रणीतः॥४॥**

**स पुण्यकीर्तिः शतमन्युकल्पो महेन्द्रलोकप्रतिमां समृद्ध्या।  
अध्यास्त सर्वर्तुमुखामयोध्यामध्यासितां ब्रह्मभिरिद्धबोधैः॥५॥**

**निर्माणदक्षस्य समीहितेषु सीमेव पद्मासनकौशलस्य।  
ऊर्ध्वस्फुरद्रत्नगभस्तिभिर्या स्थितावहस्येव पुरं मघोनः॥६॥**

**सद्रत्नमुक्ताफलवज्रभाञ्जि विचित्रधातूनि सकाननानि।  
स्त्रीभिर्युतान्यप्सरसामिवौघैर्मेरोः शिरांसीव गृहाणि यस्याम्॥७॥**

**अन्तर्निविष्टोज्ज्वलरत्नभासो गवाक्षजालैरभिनिष्पतन्त्यः।  
हिमाद्रिटङ्कादिव भान्ति यस्यां गङ्गाम्बुपातप्रतिमा गृहेभ्यः॥८॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 4. The king and the fire propitiated in a sacrifice, who were both holy, served by many learned Bráhmanas, who both pleased the gods and who were both eminent, shone for the maintenance of the earth.

 5. He, of holy fame, equal to Indra, dwelt in अयोध्या which was like the capital of Indra on account of its prosperity, which was pleasant in all seasons and which was inhabited by the Bráhmanas of sharp intellect.

 6. Which was, as it were, the climax, among all the created objects of the skilfulness of the creator, prompt in (the work of) creation, which stood, as it were laughing at the town of Indra, with its rays of jewels glittering above.

 7. The houses in which were like the summits of the mountain Meru, since they had in them diamonds, pearls and jewels, since they contained various metals, possessed groves and had women dwelling in them who were like the Apsaras on the mountain Meru.

 8. In which the rays of the brilliant jewels stored inside, issuing from the houses through the lattice-

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**धर्म्यासुः कामार्थयशस्करीषु मतासु लोकेऽधिगतासु काले।  
विद्यासु विद्वानिव सोऽभिरेम पत्नीषु राजा तिसृषूत्तमासु॥९॥**

**पुत्रीयता तेन वराङ्गनाभिरानायि विद्वान्क्रतुषु क्रियावान्।  
विपत्क्रिमज्ञानगतिर्मनस्वी मान्यो मुनिः खां पुरमृष्यशृङ्गः॥१०॥**

**ऐहिष्ट तं कारयितुं कृतात्मा ऋतुं नृपः पुत्रफलं मुनीन्द्रम्।  
ज्ञाताशयस्तस्य ततो व्यतानीत्स कर्मठः कर्म सुतानुबन्धम्॥११॥**

**रक्षांसि वेदीं परितो निरास्थदङ्गान्ययाक्षीदभितः प्रधानम्।  
शेषाण्यहौषीत्सुतसम्पदे च वरं वरेण्यो नृपतेरमार्गीत्॥१२॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

windows, appear like the flow of the waters of the Ganges on the summit of the Himalayas.

 9.The king sported with his three best wives who were righteous, who brought him fame, worldly prosperity and worldly pleasures, who were respected by the people and who were married at the proper time, like the learned man with the Vedic lore.

 10. By him desirous of a child was brought to his capital with the help of the damsels the sage Rishyasringa who was learned, versed in the practice of sacrifices, who had access to fully developed knowledge, was high minded and highly respected.

 11. The self-subdued king desired the great sage to perform the sacrifice whose fruit was a child. He who was clever in the performance of sacrifices understood the purpose of the king and performed the sacrifice resulting in male issues.

 12.The eminent sage warded off in all directions of the altar the demons**;** worshipped the secondary gods round about the principal deity**;** the rest he sacrificed to the fire for gaining sons (for the king) and sought a boon for the king.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**निष्ठां गते दत्रिमसभ्यताषे विहित्रिमे कर्मणि राजपत्न्यः।  
प्राशुर्हुतोच्छिष्टमुदारवंश्यास्तिस्रः प्रसोतुं चतुरः सुपुत्रान्॥१३॥**

**कौसल्ययासावि सुखेन रामः प्राक्केकयीतो भरतस्ततोऽभूत्।  
प्रासोष्ट शत्रुघ्नमुदारचेष्टमेका सुमित्रा सह लक्ष्मणेन॥१४॥**

**आर्चीद्द्विजातीन्परमार्थविन्दानुदेजयान्भूतगणान्न्यषेधीत्।  
विद्वानुपानेष्ट च तान्खकाले यतिर्वसिष्ठो यमिनां वरिष्ठः॥१५॥**

**वेदोऽङ्गवांस्तैरखिलोऽध्यणायि शस्त्राण्युपायंसत जित्वराणि।  
ते भिन्नवृत्तीन्यपि मानसानि समं जनानां गुणिनोऽध्यवात्सृः॥१६॥**

**ततोऽभ्यगाद्गाधिस्रुतः क्षितीन्द्रं रक्षोभिरभ्याहतकर्मवृत्तिः।  
रामं वरीतुं परिरक्षणार्थं राजार्जिहत्तं मधुपर्कपाणिः॥१७॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 13. When the sacrifice in which the Bráhmanas were gratified with gifts, and which was performed with due rites, was come to an end, the nobly born three wives of the king partook of the residual oblations to bear four good sons.

 14. To Kausalyâ was born Rāma without travails, at first**;** afterwards to Kekayí was born Bharata, and Sumitrâ gave birth to Sàtrughaа of noble actions with Lakshmana.

 15. The wise ascetic वसिष्ठ, the greatest of the ascetics, honoured the three castes obtaining the highest object of life, warded off the terrifying demons and in due time initiated them.

 16. By them were studied the whole Veda with its members**;** by them were received the invincible weapons**;** the virtuous Râma and his brothers dwelt at the same time in the hearts of the people of diversified dispositions.

 17. Afterwards the son of Gádhi, whose daily reli-

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**ऐषीः पुनर्जन्मजयाय यत्त्वं रूपादिबोधान्यवृतच्च यत्ते।  
तत्त्वान्यबुद्धाः प्रतनूनि येन ध्यानं नृपस्तच्छिवमित्यवादीत्॥१८॥**

**आख्यन्मुनिस्तस्य शिवं समाधेर्विघ्नन्ति रक्षांसि वने ऋतूंश्च।  
तानि द्विषद्वीर्यनिराकरिष्णुस्तृणेढु रामः सह लक्ष्मणेन॥१९॥**

**स शुश्रुंवांस्तद्वचनं मुमोह राजासहिष्णुः सुतविप्रयोगम्।  
अहंयुनाथ क्षितिपः शुभंयुरुचे वचस्तापसकुञ्जरेण॥२०॥**

**मया त्वपाप्थाः शरणं भयेषु वयं त्वयाप्याप्स्महि धर्मवृद्ध्यै।  
क्षात्रं द्विजत्वं च परस्परार्थं शङ्कां कृथा मा प्रहिणु स्वसूनुम्॥२१॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

gious performances were disturbed by the demons, came to the king to ask for Ráma for their protection**;** the king with मधुपर्क in his hand worshipped him**.**

** **18. The king said **"**Is that meditation of yours without disturbance**,** which you have desired for the conquest of rebirth**,** which you have turned away from the consciousness of the**.** Gunas**,** and by which you have understood the subtle elements?**"**

** **19. The Muni replied**, “**It is well with my meditations, (but) the demons disturb (my) sacrifices in the forest**;** let Ráma**,** able to nullify the prowess of (his enemies)**,** with Lakshman destroy them**.”**

** **20. The king**,** unable to bear the separation of his son**'**s fainted at the hearing of his (sage's) words**,** the blessed king was addressed the (following) words by the eminent hermit**.**

** **21. **“**In my dangers thou hast been approached by me for protection**,** we are approached by thee for the increase of religious merit**;** (thus) heroism (**क्षात्रं**) and Brahmanism are for mutual assistance**;** entertain no doubt**,** send your son**."**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**घानिष्यते तेन महान्विपक्षः स्थायिष्यते येन रणे पुरस्तात्।  
मा मां महात्मन्परिभूर योग्ये न मद्विधो न्यस्यति भारमग्र्यम्॥२२॥**

** क्रुध्यन्कुलं धक्ष्यति विप्रवह्निर्यास्यन्सुतस्तप्स्यति मां समन्युम्।  
इत्थं नृपःपूर्वमवालुलोचे ततोऽनुजज्ञे गमनं सुतस्य॥२३॥**

**आशीर्भिरभ्यर्च्य ततः क्षितीन्द्रं प्रीतः प्रतस्थे मुनिराश्रमाय।  
तं पृष्ठतः प्रष्ठमियाय नम्रो हिंस्रेपुदीप्ताप्तधनुः कुमारः॥२४॥**

**प्रयास्यतः पुण्यवनाय जिष्णोरामस्य राचिष्णुमुखस्य धृष्णुः।  
त्रैमातुरः कृत्स्नजितास्त्रशस्त्रः सध्र्यङ् रतः श्रेयसि लक्ष्मणोऽभूत्॥२५॥**

**इषुमति रघुसिंहे दन्दशूकाञ्जिघांसौ  
 धनुररिभिरसद्यं मुष्टिपीडं दधाने।**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 22. By him will be killed the great enemy who will oppose him in the battle**;** do not disregard me**,** O highsouled one**;** men of my type do not place their highest (अग्र्य) reliance on improper objects**.**

** **23. Thus did the king think beforehand **“**the fire like Bra**'**hmin will consume my race if he get angry**,** the son by going will grieve my sorrowful person**."** Then he permitted the departure of his son**.**

** **24. Then the pleased Muni**,** having worshipped the king with blessings**,** set out for his hermitage**;** the humble youth (Ráma) with a brilliant and unfailing bow and deadly arrows followed him who was going before (in front)**.**

** **25. Lakshman**,** the son of the three mothers**,** wellversed in the science of missiles and weapons**,** and devoted to his welfare became the companion of Ra'ma who was going to the holy forest**,** who loved to conquer**,** whose face was blooming and who was brave**.**

** **26. . When the best of Raghus**,** (Ráma)**,** who desired

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**व्रजति पुरतरुण्यो बद्धचित्राङ्गुलित्रे  
 कथ्मपि गुरुशोकान्मा रुदन् माङ्गलिक्यः॥२३॥**

**अथ जगदुनीचैराशिषस्तस्य विप्रा-  
 स्तुमुलकलनिनादं तूर्यमाजघ्रुरन्ये।  
अभिमतफलशंसी चारु पुस्फोर बाहु-  
स्तरुषु चुकुवुरुच्चैः पक्षिणश्चानुकूलाः॥२७॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

to kill the demons, who held a bow unbearable to the enemies and pressing against the fist and who wore the finger-protector, was going, the auspicious young women of the town withheld their weeping with great difficulty.

 27. Then the Vipras addressed him blessings very loudly, others played on the various musical instruments of loud and sweet notes and the arm indicating a desired fruit throbbed greatly, and the auspicious birds warbled their notes in distinct voice.

**इति भट्टिकाव्ये रामोत्पत्तिर्नाम प्रथमः सर्गः।**

**———————**

**द्वितीयः सर्गः**  
**———————**

**वनस्पतीनां सरसां नदीनां तेजस्विनां कान्तिभृतां दिशाञ्च।  
निर्याय तस्याः स पुरः समन्तच्छ्रियं दधानां शरदं ददर्श॥१॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 1. He, baving come out of the town of Ayodhyâ observed on all sides the autumn, bearing the beauty of trees, of lakes, of rivers and of resplendent and clear quarters.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**तरङ्गमङ्गाच्चपलैः पलाशैर्ज्वालाश्रियं सातिशयां दधन्ति।  
सधूमदीप्ताग्रिरुचीनि रेजूस्ताम्रोत्पलान्याकुलषट्पदानि॥२॥ **

**विम्बागतैस्तीरवनैः समृद्धिं निजां विलोक्यापहृतां पयोभिः।  
कूलानि सामर्षतयेव तेनुः सरोजलक्ष्मीं स्थलपद्महासैः॥३॥**

**निशातुषारैर्नयनाम्बुकल्पैः पत्रान्तपर्यगलदच्छबिन्दुः।  
उपारुरोदेव नदत्पतङ्गः कुमुद्वतीं तीरतरुर्दिनादौ॥४॥**

**वनानि तोयानि च नेत्रकल्पेः पृष्यैः सरोजैश्च निलीनभृङ्गैः।  
परस्परां विस्पयवान्ति लक्ष्मीमालोकयाञ्चक्रुरिवादरेण॥५॥**

**प्रभातवाताहतिकम्पिताकृतिः कुमुद्वतीरेणुपिशङ्गविग्रहम्‌।  
निराश भृङ्गं कुपितेव पद्मनी न मानिनी संसदहतेऽन्यसङ्गपम्‌॥६॥ **

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 2. The red lotuses, bearing the lustre of the glowing fire accompanied by smoke and possessing the excessive splendour of the flames by their petals (gently) moved by their contact with the waves and having the humming bees on them, shone brightly.

 3. Obserbving that their own splendour has been carried away by the waters by means of the trees reflected in them, the banks, through jealousy as it were, spread the splendour of the water-lotuses, by means of that of the land-lotuses.

 4. At the beginning of the day the tree on the; bank, on whom the birds warbled and from the ends of the leaves of which dew drops were falling down, wept for the water-lily with dew drops resembling tears.

 5. The forests and waters filled with wonder looked, as it were, through respect at one another’s beauty with eye-like flowers and lotuses respectively.

 6. The Padmini, like an angry woman, with her body trembling with the morning breeze, rejects the bee with  


**दत्तावधानं मधुलेहिगीतौ प्रशान्तचेष्टं हरिणं जिघांसुः।  
आकर्णयन्नुत्सुकहंसनादान् लक्ष्ये समाधिं न दधे मृगावित्॥७॥**

**गिरेर्नितम्बे मरुता विभिन्नं तोयावशेषेण हिमाभमभ्रम्।  
सरिन्मुखाभ्युच्चयमादधानं शैलाधिपस्यानुचकार लक्ष्मीम्॥८॥**

**गर्जन् हरिः साम्भसि शैलकुञ्जे प्रतिध्वनीनात्मकृतान्निशम्य।  
क्रमं बबन्ध क्रमितुं सकोपः प्रतर्कयन्नन्यमृगेन्द्रनादान्॥९॥**

**अदृताम्भांसि नवोत्पलानि रुतानि चाश्रोषत षट्पदानाम्।  
आघ्रायि वान् गन्धवहः सुगन्धस्तेनारविन्दव्यतिषङ्गवांश्च॥१०॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

his body made tawny with the pollen dust of the Kumudvati; a jealous woman does not bear (her husband's) union with another.

 7. The hunter wishing to kill the deer who has fixed his attention on the sweet buzzing of bees and whose movements were (for a time) at a stand still, did not fix his attention on his aim, as he heard the notes of the intoxicated cranes or swans.

 8. The cloud, on the ridge of a mountain, divided by wind, (therefore) resembling dew on account of the residual waters and increasing the source of the rivers, appeared to imitate the Himalayas in point of beauty.

 9. The roaring lion, hearing the echoes made by him in the mountain cave full of water, prepared himself to jump in anger, suspecting them to be the roars of other lions

 10. He saw waters full of lotuses; the buzz of bees he heard; by him was enjoyed the wind sweet scented, bearing the fragrance and coming in contact with the lotuses.

**लतानुपातं कुसुमान्यगृह्णात्स नद्यवस्कन्दमुपास्टशच्च।  
कुतूहलाच्चारुशिलोपवेशं काकुत्स्थ ईषत्स्मयमान आस्त॥११॥**

**तिग्मांशुरश्मिच्छुरितान्यदूरात्प्राञ्चि प्रभाते सलिलान्यपश्यत्।  
गभस्तिधाराभिरिव द्रुतानि तेजांसि भानोर्भुवि सम्भृतानि॥१२॥**

**दिग्व्यापिनीर्लोचनलोभनीया मृजान्वयाः स्नेहमिव स्रवन्तीः।  
ऋज्वायताः शस्यविशेषपङ्क्तीस्तुतोष पश्यन् वितृणान्तरालाः॥१३॥**

**वियोगदुःखानुभवानभिज्ञैः काले नृपांशं विहितं ददद्भिः।  
आहार्यशोभारहितैरमायैरैक्षिष्ट पुम्भिः प्रचितान् स गोष्ठान्॥१४॥**

**स्त्रीभूषणं चेष्टितमप्रगल्भं  
 चारुण्यवक्राण्यपि वीक्षितानि।**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 11. Through curiosity Râma with a gentle laugh gathered flowers going to one creaper after another; sipped the water, descending into this river and that; and sat on one beautiful stone after another.

 12. At the dawn, he saw in the east waters brightened by the rays of the sun, not far off, as if they were the lustre of the sun melted through the rays and deposited on the earth.

 13. He (Râma) was pleased to see the rows of various corn, occupying the quarters, fascinating to the eyes, very pure, and dropping down, as it were, love; straight and tall and with grass cleared away from their midst.

 14. He eyed the cow-pens inhabited by the cow-herds who were healthy, without any artificial splendour, who paid at the proper time the imposed taxes and who never experienced the sorrow of separation.

 15. He was delighted to see the bashful movements, which are an ornament to women, the beautiful

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**ऋजूंश्च विश्वासकृतः स्वभावान्  
 गोपाङ्गनानां मुमुदे विलोक्य॥१५॥**

**विवृत्तपार्श्वं रुचिराङ्गहारं समुद्रहच्चारुनितम्बरम्यम्।  
आमन्द्रमन्थध्वनिदत्ततालं गोपाङ्गनानृत्यमनन्दयत्तम्॥१६॥**

**विचित्रमुचैः प्लवमानमारात् कुतूहलं त्रस्तु ततान तस्य।  
मेघासयोपात्तवनोपशोभं कदम्बकं वातमजं मृगाणाम्॥१७॥**

**सितारविन्दप्रचयेषु लीनाः संसक्तफेणेषु च सैकतेषु।  
कुन्दावदाताः कलहंसमालाः प्रतीयिरे श्रोत्रसुखैर्निनादैः॥१८॥**

** न तज्जलं यन्न सुचारु पङ्कजं  
    न पङ्कजं तद्यदलीनषट्पदम्।  
  न षट्पदोऽसौ न जुगुञ्ज यः कलं  
     न गुञ्जितं तन्न जहार यन्मनः॥१९॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

though straight looks and the innocent and confident natures of the herdswomen.

 16. The dance of the herdswomen pleased him— in which the sides turned to and fro, in which there were graceful movements of the limbs, which appeared beautiful on account of the graceful hips moving backwards and forwards and in which time was kept by the rumbling sound of churning.

 17. The herd of deer, timid, jumping high near him, variegated and come to the beautiful forest after the disappearance of the clouds, and running in the direction of the wind, enhanced his curiosity.

 18. The swans, settling on the white lotuses and on the foamy sand-banks and white as the Kunda flowers, were recognised (by Ráma) by means of their notes pleasant to the ears.

 19. There was no water (which Ráma eyed) in

**तं यायजूकाः सह भिक्षुपुख्यैस्तपः कृशा शान्त्युदकुम्भहस्ताः।  
यायावराः पुष्पफलेन चान्ये प्राणर्चरर्च्या जगदर्चनीयम्॥२०॥**

**विद्यामथैनं विजयां जयां च  
 रक्षोगणं क्षिप्नुमविक्षतात्मा।  
अध्यापिपद्गाधिसुतो यथाव  
 न्निघातयिष्यन् युधि यातुधानान्॥२१॥**

**आयोधने स्थायुकमस्त्रजातममोघमभ्यर्णमहाहवाय।  
ददौ वधाय क्षणदाचराणां तस्मै मुनिः श्रेयसि जागरूकः॥२२॥**

**तं विप्रदर्श कृतघातयत्ना यान्तं वने रात्रिचरी डुढौके।  
जिघांसुवेदं धृतभासुरास्त्रस्तां ताडकाख्यां निजघान रामः॥२३॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

which there were no beautiful lotuses; there was not a lotus on which not a bee alighted; there was not a bee which did not buzz; there was no buzzing which did not fascinate his mind.

 20. The venerable sacrificers, reduced through austerities, with propitiatory water-pots in their hands and in company of the chief mendicants, worshipped him (by sprinkling water on him); other venerable mendicants worshipped him, with flowers and fruits.

 21. The son of Gádhi, of a tranquil mind and desirous to kill the demons in a fight, taught with due ceremony the military science, which will kill the demons, to Râma.

 22. To him who was soon to be engaged in battle, the sage, mindful of his welfare, gave a number of missiles, steady and never failing in battle, for the destruction of the demons.

 23. A female demon, who made efforts to kill every Bráhmana she saw, approached him who

**अथालुलोके हुतधूमकेतुशिखाञ्जनास्निग्धसमद्धशाखम्‌।  
तपोवनं प्राध्ययनाभिभूतसमुच्चरच्चारुपतत्रिशिञ्जम्॥**

**क्षुद्रान्न जक्षुर्हरिणान् मृगेन्द्रा विशश्वसे पक्षिगणैः समन्तात्‌।  
नन्नम्यमानाः फलदित्सयेव चकाशिरे तत्र लता विलोलाः॥२५॥**

**अपूपुञ्जन्विष्टरपाद्यमाल्यैरातिथ्यनिष्णा वनवासिमुख्याः।**  
**प्रत्यग्रहीष्ठां मधुपर्कमिश्रं तावासनादि क्षितिपालपुत्रौ॥२६॥**

**दैत्याभिभूतस्य यूवामवोढं मग्नस्य दोर्भिर्भुवनस्य भारम्‌।  
हवींषि सम्प्रत्यपि रक्षतं तौ तपोधनैरित्थमपभाषिषाताम्॥२७**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

was going to the forest; Rama with a burning weapon in his hand, destroyed her who intended to kill (him).

 24. Then he saw the penance-grove, the boughs (of the trees) in which were besmeared with the pigment-like smoke of the blazing sacrificial fire and which the sweetly warbled notes of the birds were excelled by the reading (of the Vedas)

 25. There the lords of the beasts did not kill the petty deer; the birds on all sides confided on one another; and the trembling creepers, bending low, as if, with a desire to offer fruit (to the sages) looked beautiful there.

 26. The chief of the forest-dwellers received them with flowers, water and seats; the two sons of the king accepted the seats &c. with the mixture of honey.

  27. Thus were they addressed by the sages, “You sustained the weight of the earth troubled by the demons and (therefore) sunk in misfortune; even now do you protect our oblations.”

**तान् प्रत्यवादीदथ राघवोऽपि यथेप्सितं प्रस्तुत कर्म धर्म्यम्।  
तपोमरुद्भिर्भवतां शराग्निः सन्धुक्ष्यतां नोऽरिसमिन्धनेषु ॥२८॥**

**प्रतुष्टुवुः कर्म ततः प्रक्लृप्तैस्ते यज्ञियैर्द्रव्यमणैर्यथावत्।  
दक्षिण्यदिष्टं कृतमार्त्विजीनैस्तद्यातुधानैश्चिचिते प्रसर्पत्॥२९॥**

**आपिङ्गरुक्षोर्ध्वशिरस्यचालैः शिरालजङ्घैर्गिर्रिकूटदघ्नैः।  
ततः क्षपाटैः पृथुपिङ्गलाक्षैः खं प्रावृषेण्यैरिव चानशेऽब्दैः॥३०॥**

**अधिज्यचापः स्थिरबाहुमृष्टिरुदञ्चिताक्षोऽञ्चितदक्षिणोरुः।  
तान् लक्ष्मणः सन्नतवामजङ्को जघान शुद्धेषुरमन्दकर्षी॥३१॥**

**गाधेयदिष्टं विरसं रसन्तं रामोऽपि मायाचणमस्त्रचुञ्चुः।  
स्थास्नुं रणे स्मेरमुखो जगाद मारीचमुच्चैर्वचनं महार्थम्॥३२॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 28 .To them replied even Râma afterwards, "Begin the religious work (sacrifice) as desired (by you). Let our fire-like arrows be inflamed by your wind-like austerities over the fuel-like enemies.

 29. They commenced the sacrifice with materials fit to be offered; the sacrifice performed by the priests as directed by the venerable Munis, was noticed by the demons while it was being carried on, (प्रसर्पन्).

 30.The sky was filled with the demons, as black as the rain clouds, the hairs of whose heads were erect, rough and tawny; whose joints were full of veins; and who were as tall as the summit of a mountain; and whose eyes were broad and tawny.

 31. Lakshmaṇa, his bow-string well stretched, his arm and fist steady, his eyes uplifted, his right thigh bent down, his left joint bent, his arrows being well pointed, killed them all, (after) drawing his bow forcibly.

 32. Râma too, with a gentle smile on his lips,  
\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**आत्मम्भरिस्वं पिशितैर्नराणां फलेग्रहीन् हंसि वनस्पतीनाम्‌।  
शौवस्तिकत्वं विभवा न येषां व्रजन्ति तेषां दयसे न कस्मात्‌॥३३॥**

**अद्मो द्विजान्‌ देवयजीन्निहन्मः कुर्मः पुरं प्रेतनराधिवासम्।  
धर्मो ह्ययं दाशरथे निजो नो नैवाध्यकारिष्महि वेदवृत्ते॥३४॥**

**धर्मोऽस्ति सत्यं तव राक्षसायमन्यो व्यतिस्ते तु ममापि धर्मः।  
ब्रह्माद्विषस्थे प्रणिहन्मि येन राजन्यवृत्तिर्धृतकार्मुकेषुः॥३५॥**

**इत्थम्प्रवादं युधि सम्प्रहारं प्रचक्रतृ रामनिशाविहारौ।  
तृणाय मत्वा रघुनन्दनोऽथ बाणेन रक्षः प्रधनान्निरास्थत्‌॥३६॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

skilled in the use of missiles addressed loudly the (following) words, full of meaning, to Máricha, pointed out by Gádheya, mumbling, noted for jugglery and fit to remain on the battle-field,

 33. “Thou, living on the flesh of men, killest those that maintain themselves with the fruits of trees: Why dost thou not protect those whose provisions are ephemeral.”

 34. (The demon replied) ‘‘We eat the Dwijas, kill the worshippers of the gods and (thus) make our town the abode of the dead. O son of Das‘aratha! this is our constant duty ; we have not been given a right to the life enjoined by the Vedas.”

 35. “O demon! verily this is your duty, but (अपितु) on the other hand there is another duty of mine which out-weighs yours; by virtue of that duty I shall kill thee hater of the Bráhmanas; (since) I am a Kshatriya and a holder of a bow and arrows.”

 36. On the battlefield Râma and the night-wanderer engaged into a conflict of words; then the descendant of Raghu, treating him with contempt drove

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**जग्मुः प्रसादं द्विजमानसानि द्यौर्वर्षुका पुष्पचयं बभूव।  
निर्व्याजमिज्या ववृते वचश्च भूयो बभाषे मुनिना कुमारः॥३७॥**

**महीय्यमाना भवतातिमात्रं सुराध्वरे घस्मरजित्वरेण।  
दिवोऽपि वज्रायुधभूषणाया ह्रिणीयते वीरवती न भूमिः॥३८॥**

**बलिर्बबन्धे जलधिर्ममन्थे जहेऽमृतं दैत्यकुलं विजिग्ये।  
कल्पान्तदुःस्था वसुधा तथाहि येनैष भारोऽतिगुरुर्न तस्य॥३९॥**

**इति ब्रुवाणो मधुरं हितं च तमाञ्जिहन्मैथिलयज्ञभूमिम्।  
रामं मुनिः प्रीतमना मखान्ते यशांसि राज्ञां निजिघृक्षयष्यन्॥४०॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

the Rakshasa away from the battle-ground with an arrow.

 37. The minds of the sages were satisfied; the heavens became a showerer of flowers; The sacrifice was performed securely; the boy was again addressed by the sage.

 38. “The earth, possessing heroes (of thy type) and greatly exalted by thee, the destroyer of the demons in sacrifices to the gods, is not surpassed (in glory) by the Paradise whose ornament is Indra."

 39. "This task is not difficult to him by whom Bali was bound, the ocean was churned, the nectar was seized, the family of demons was conquered, and the earth, when badly circumstanced, was borne up.

 40. The sage, who spoke, in this way, sweet and agreeable words, who was inwardly pleased and who wished to have the fame of the kings obscured (by Râma), took : Râma to Mithilá, the sacrificial ground of king Mithila, after the completion of the sacrifice.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**इतः स्म मित्रावरुणौ किमेतौ किमश्विनौ सोमरसं पिपासु।  
जनं समस्तं जनकाश्रमस्थं रूपेण तावौजिहतां नृसिंहौ॥४१॥**

**अजिग्रहत्तं जनको धनुस्तद्येनार्दिदहैयपुरं पिनाकी।  
जिज्ञासमानो बलमस्य बाह्वोर्हसन्नभाङ्क्षीद्रघुनन्दनस्तत्॥४२॥**

**ततो नदीष्णान्‌ पथिकान्‌  
 गिरिज्ञानाह्वायकान्‌ भूमिपतेरयोध्याम्‌।  
दित्सुः सुतां योधहररैस्तुरङ्गैर्व्य-  
 सर्जयन्मैथिलमर्त्यमुख्यः॥४३॥**

**क्षिप्रं ततोऽध्वन्यतुरङ्गयायी यविष्ठवग्वृद्धतमो5पि राजा।  
आख्यायकेभ्यः श्रुतसूनुवृत्तिरग्लानयानो मिथिलामगच्छत्‌॥४४॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 41. On account of their beautiful appearance they caused all the people of the hermitage of Janaka to doubt whether they were Mitrãvaruna or \।the twin-gods Aświns desirous of drinking soma juice.

 42. Janaka, desirous to ascertain the strength of his (Râma’s) arm, gave him that bow with which the God Siva had broken up the town of the demons; the descendant of Raghu, with a gentle laugh (or easily) broke it up.

 43. Then the chief of the Mithilá people, wishing to give his daughter (to Râma in marriage) despatched messengers, with brave horses, who were clever swimmers, way-farers, acquainted with mountains (paths), to Ayodhyã to the king Daśaratha,

 44. Then the king, who drove in a coach of speedy horses, who acted like the most youthful, though very old, and who came to know of the conduct of his son from the informers, went hastily to Mithilá.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_  


**जग्मुः प्रसादं द्विजमानसानि द्यौर्वर्षुका पुष्पचयं बभूव।  
निर्व्याजमिज्या ववृते वचश्च भूयो बभाषे मुनिना कुमारः॥३७॥**

**महीय्यमाना भवतातिमात्रं सुराध्वरे घस्मरजित्वरेण।  
दिवोऽपि वज्रायुधभूषणाया ह्रीणीयते वीरवती न भूमिः॥३८॥**

**बलिर्बबन्धे जलधिर्ममन्थे जह्रेऽमृतं दैत्यकुलं विजिग्ये।  
कल्पान्तदुःस्था वसुधा तथाहि येनैष भारोऽतिगुरुर्न तस्य॥३९॥**

**इति ब्रुवाणो मधुरं हितं च तमाञ्जिहन्मैथिलयज्ञभूमिम्।  
रामं मुनिः प्रीतमना मखान्ते यशांसि राज्ञां निजिघृक्षयष्यन्॥४०॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

the Rákshasa away from the battle-ground with an arrow.

 37. The minds of the sages were satisfied; the heavens became a showerer of flowers; The sacrifice was performed securely; the boy was again addressed by the sage.

 38. “The earth, possessing heroes (of thy type) and greatly exalted by thee, the destroyer of the demons in sacrifices to the gods, is not surpassed (in glory) by the Paradise whose ornament is Indra."

 39. "This task is not difficult to him by whom Bali was bound, the ocean was churned, the nectar was seized, the family of demons was conquered, and the earth, when badly circumstanced, borne up."

 40. The sage, who spoke, in this way, sweet and agreeable words, who was inwardly pleased and who wished to have the fame of the kings obscured (by Râma), took · Râma to Mithilá, the sacrificial ground of king Mithila, after the completion of the sacrifice.  
\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

**इतः स्म मित्रावरुणौ किमेतौ किमश्विनौ सोमरसं पिपासू।  
जनं समस्तं जनकाश्रमस्थं रूपेण तावौजिहतां नृसिंहौ॥४१॥**

**अजिग्रहत्तं जनको धनुस्तद्येनार्दिदद्दैत्यपुरं पिनाकी।  
जिज्ञासमानो बलपस्य बाह्वोर्हसन्नभाङ्क्षीद्रघुनन्दनस्तत्॥४२॥**

**ततो नदीष्णान् पथिकान्  
 गिरिज्ञानाह्वायकान् भूमिपतेरयोध्याम्।  
दित्सुः सुतां योधहरैस्तुरङ्गैर्व्य-  
 सर्जयन्मैथिलमर्त्यमुख्यः॥४३॥**

**क्षिप्रं ततोऽध्वन्यतुरङ्गयायी यविष्ठवद्वृद्धतमोऽपि राजा।  
आख्यायकेभ्यः श्रुतसूनुवृत्तिरग्लानयानो मिथिलामगच्छत्॥४४॥**

———————————————————————————————————————————

 41. On account of their beautiful appearance they caused all the people of the hermitage of Janaka to doubt whether they were Mitrãvaruṇa or the twin-gods As'wins desirous of drinking soma juice.

 42. Janaka, desirous to ascertain the strength of his (Râma's) arm, gave him that bow with which the God Síva had broken up the town of the demons; the descendant of Raghu, with a gentle laugh (or easily) broke it up.

 43. Then the chief of the Mithilã people, wishing to give his daughter (to Râma in marriage) despatched messengers, with brave horses, who were clever swimmers, way-farers, acquainted with mountains (paths), to Ayodhya to the king Das'aratha.

 44. Then the king, who drove in a coach of speedy horses, who acted like the most youthful, though very old, and who came to know of the conduct of his son from the informers, went hastily to Mithila.

————————————————————————————————————————————

**वृन्दिष्टमार्चीद्वसुधाधिपानां तं प्रेष्ठमेतं गुरुवद्गरिष्ठुम्।  
सदृङ्महान्तं सुकृताधिवासं वंहिष्ठकीर्तिं यशसा वरिष्ठम्॥१५॥**

**त्रिवर्गपारीणमसौ भवन्तमध्यासयन्नासनमेकमिन्द्रः।  
विवेकदृश्वत्वमगात्सुराणां तं मैथिलो वाक्यमिदं बभाषे॥४६॥**

**हिरण्मयी साललतेव जङ्गमा  
 च्युतादिवः स्थास्नुरिवानिप्रभा।  
शशाङ्ककान्तेरधिदेवताकृतिः  
 सुता ददे तस्य सुताय मैथिली॥४७॥**

**लब्धां ततो विश्वजनीनवृत्तिस्तामात्मनीनामुदवोढ रामः।  
सद्रत्नमुक्ताफलभर्मशोभां संवंहयन्तीं रघुवर्ग्यलक्ष्मीम्॥१८॥**

———————————————————————————————

 45. Janaka worshipped him who was most excellent, highly beloved by the kings, who was most high like Guru, highly illustrious among his colleagues, goodly attired, of wide fame and foremost by his glory.

 46. The king of Mithilá addressed him thus, "Indra, asking you, who have fulfilled the three objects of worldly existence, to occupy his own throne, showed great discernment.

 47 The daughter Maithili was given in marriage to Râma who —— was as active as the golden Săla creeper, who was like the steady flash of lightning slipped from the sky and who was the presiding deity of the moon's beauty. in

 48. Râma, devoted to the good of the whole universe, afterwards married her who was made over to him, who was worthy of him and who bore the splendour of the golden ornaments, pearls and jewels and who was the splendour of the Raghu race.

—————————————————————————————————————————————

**सुप्रातमासादितसम्मदं तद्वन्दारुभिः संस्तुतमभ्ययोध्यम्।  
उमश्वीयराजन्यकहास्तिकाढ्यमगात्सराजं बलमध्वनीनम्॥१९॥**

**विशङ्कटो वक्षसि बाणपाणिः सम्पन्नतालद्वयसः पुरस्तात्।  
भीष्मो धनुष्मानुपजान्वरत्रिरैतिस्म रामं पथि जामदग्न्यः॥५०॥**

**उच्चैरसौ राघवमाह्वतेदं धनुः सबाणं कुरु मातियासीः।  
पराक्रमज्ञः प्रियसन्ततिस्तं नम्रः क्षितीन्द्रोऽनुनिनीषुरूचे॥५१॥**

**अनेकशो निर्जितराजकस्त्वं पितॄनतासीर्नृपरक्ततोयैः।  
सङ्क्षिप्य संरम्भमसद्विपक्षं कास्थार्भकेऽस्मिंस्तव राम ! रामे॥५२॥**

———————————————————————————————————————

 49. In the early morning the army, with the king, able to undertake a long journey, highly pleased, whose praises were sung by his (Janaka's) bards and which consisted of elephants, princes and horses, set out to Ayodhyá.

 50. On the way the dreadful son of Jamadagni broad in his chest, with an arrow in his hand, as tall as a fullgrown palm-tree, with a bow and having his arms as long as to reach the knees, encountered Râma in front.

 51. Loudly did he challenge Rághava with these words, “Fit the bow with an arrow, do not go away. The king, who knew of his prowess and to whom the child was very dear, wishing to appease him, said humbly (नम्रः सन्).

 52. "In various ways art thou the conqueror of the fraternity of the kings; thou hast appeased thy manes by offering to them in the form of the blood of the kings; away with thy anger which has no enemy; O Râma, why should thou mind Râma, a mere boy ?”

—————————————————————————————————————————

**अजीगणद्दाशरथं न वाक्यं यदा स दर्पेण तदा कुमारः।  
धनुर्त्र्यकार्ङ्क्षीद्गुरुवाणगर्भं लोकानलावीद्विजितांश्च तस्य॥५३॥**

**जिते नृपारौ सुमनीभवन्ति शब्दायमानान्यशनैरशङ्कम्।  
वृद्धस्य राज्ञोऽनुमते बलानि जगाहिरेऽनेकमुखानि मार्गान् ॥५४॥**

**अथ पुरुजवयोगान्नेदयद्दूरसंस्थं  
 दवयदतिरयेण प्राप्तमुर्वीविभागम्।  
क्लमरहितमचेतन्नीरजीकारितक्ष्मां  
 बलमुपहितशोभां तूर्णमायादयोध्याम्॥५५॥**

——————————————————————————————————————————

 53. When he, through pride, did not mind the words of Das'aratha, the boy stretched his bow fitted with a mighty arrow and destroyed the Lokas conquered by Paras'aráma.

 54. When the king's enemy was conquered, his soldiers, whose minds were set at ease and who produced a terrible sound, went fearlessly their way, with the consent of the old king, in various groups.

 55. Afterwards the army, bringing near through its great speed the part of the earth which is distant and making distant that, which has come near, through its great velocity, and being unfatigued quickly reached Ayodhya the ground of which was watered and which was decorated with festoons &c.

**इति भट्टिकाव्ये प्रकीर्णकाण्डे सीतापरिणयो नाम  
द्वितीयः सर्गः।  
≈≈≈≈≈≈≈≈**

**तृतीयः सर्गः ।  
≈≈≈≈≈≈≈**

**बधेन सङ्ख्ये पिशिताशनानां क्षत्रान्तकस्याभिभवेन चैव।  
आढ्यम्भविष्णुर्यशसा कुमारः प्रियम्भविष्णुर्न स यस्य नासीत्॥१॥**

**ततः सुचेतीकृतपौरभृत्यो राज्येऽभिषेक्ष्ये सुतमित्यनीचैः।  
आघोषयन् भूमिपतिः समस्तं भूयोऽपि लोकं सुमनीचकार॥२॥**

**आदिक्षदादीप्तकृशानुकल्पं सिंहासनं तस्य सपादपीठम्।  
सन्तप्तचामीकरवल्गुवज्रं विभागविन्यस्तमहार्घरत्नम्॥३॥**

**प्रास्थापयत्पूगकृतान् स्वपोषं पृष्ठान् प्रयत्नादृढगात्रबन्धान्।  
सभर्मकुम्भान् पुरुषान् समन्तात्पत्काषिणस्तीर्थजलार्थमाशु॥४॥**

—————————————————————————————————————————————

 1. There was nobody to whom the boy, who was attaining to eminence on account of his glory (won) after the destruction of the demons in the battle and after the defeat of the destroyer of the Kshatriyas, was not becoming an object of love and esteem.

 2. The king, who had pacified the minds of his subjects and servants, again put their minds at rest by proclaiming aloud, "I shall install my son on the throne."

 3. He ordered for him (Râma) a throne, with a foot-stool, as brilliant as fire, set with beautiful diamonds and well-polished gold and with precious jewels in the intervals.

 4. Immediately he despatched, with great care, men, with gold vessels, formed into various bands, nourished on his food and strongly built in body and inured to hard walking, in all directions to bring holy water.

**उक्षान् प्रचक्रुर्नगरस्य मार्गान् ध्वजान् बबन्धुर्मुमुचुः खधूपान्।  
दिशश्च पुष्पैश्चकरुर्विचित्रैरर्थेषु राज्ञा निपुणा नियुक्ताः॥५॥**

**मातामहावासमुपेयिवांसं मोहादष्टष्ट्वा भरतं तदानीम्।  
तत्केकयी सोढुमशक्नुवाना ववार रामस्य वतप्रयाणम्॥६॥**

**कर्णेजपैराहितराज्यलोभा स्त्रैणेन नीता विकृतिं लघिम्ना।  
राममवासे व्यमृशन्न दोषं जनापवादं सनरेन्द्रमृत्युम्॥७॥**

**वसूनि देशांश्च निवर्तयिष्यन्रामं नृपः सङ्गिरमाण एव।  
तयावजज्ञे भरताभिषेको विषादशङ्कुश्च मतौ निचख्ने॥८॥**

**ततः प्रविव्राजयिषुः कुमारमादिक्षदस्याभिगमं वनाय।  
सौमित्रिसीतानुचरस्य राजा सुमन्त्रनेत्रेण रथेन शोचन्॥९॥**

—————————————————————————————————————————————

 5. The experts, who were appointed by the king to work, sprinkled the roads of the capital with water, raised flags, discharged rockets and covered the quarters with flowers of various kinds.

 6. Then Kekayi, who was unable to bear it, asked for the banishment to forest of Râma without consulting Bharata, who was residing at his grand-father's, through infatuation or blindness.

 7. Made covetous of the throne by the traducers and perverted by feminine meanness of mind she did not consider her fault involving public censure and the death of the king.

 8. The king, who promised wealth and countries with a desire to bring back Râma, was disregarded by her; she set her heart on the coronation of Bharata and considered her disappointment as a dart (thrust in her heart).

 9. The grief-stricken king, wishing to send (his) son (to the forest) ordered his departure to the

**केचिन्निनिन्दुर्नृपमप्रशान्तं विचुक्रुशः केचन सास्रमुच्चैः।  
ऊचुस्तथान्ये भरतस्य मायां धिक्केकयीमित्यपरो जगाद॥१०॥**

**गतो वनं श्वोभवितेति रामः शोकेन देहे जनतातिमात्रम्।  
धीरास्तु तत्र च्युतमन्यवोऽन्ये दधुः कुमारानुगमे मनांसि ॥११॥**

**प्रस्थास्यमानावृपसेदुपस्तौ शोशुच्यमानामिदमूचतुस्तान्।  
किंशोचतेहाभ्युदये बतास्मान् नियोगलाभेन पितुः कृतार्थान् ॥१२॥**

**असृष्ट यो यश्च भयेष्वरक्षीद्यः सर्वदास्मान्नपृषत्स्वपोषम्।  
महोपकारस्य किमस्ति तस्य तुच्छेन यानेन वनस्य मोक्षः॥१३॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

forest with the son of Sumitrá (लक्ष्मण) and Sitá for his companions in the chariot the eye of which was Sumantra.

 10. Some censured the uxorious king, others wept aloud with tears (in their eyes), some said that it was a political trick of Bharata and others again said, "Fie upon Kekayi."

 11. The people were, burnt with grief at the thought that Râma would go to the forest tomorrow while (तु) the wise among them, shaking off all grief set their hearts upon accompanying the boy.

 12. When about to set out they (both) addressed those, who had approached them and had been weeping, these words, “Wonder ! you mourn for us happy with the fulfilment of our father's command at the time of our good fortune."

 13. "Is there any (adequate) discharge, by the trivial departure to forest, of the great obligation of him who gave us life, protected us from dangers and always fed us with his food (i.e. spent his wealth for our maintenance).

**विद्युत्प्रणाशं स वरं प्रनष्टो यद्वोर्ध्वशोषं तृणवद्विशुष्कः।  
अर्थे दुरापे किमुत प्रवासे न शासनेऽवास्थित यो गुरूणाम्॥११॥**

**पौरा निवर्त्तध्वमिति न्यमादीत्तातस्य शोकापनुदा भवेत।  
मा दर्शतान्यं भरतं च मत्तो निवर्तयेत्याह रथं स्म मूतम्॥१५॥**

**ज्ञात्वेगितैर्गत्वरतां जनानामेकां शयित्वा रजनीं सपौरः।  
रक्षन् वनेवासकृताद्भयात्तान् प्रातश्छलेनापजगाम रामः॥१६॥**

**अस्राक्षुरस्रं करुणं रुवन्तो मुहुर्मुहुर्न्यश्वसिषुः कवोष्णम्।  
हा राम हा कष्टमिति ब्रुवन्तः पराङ्मुखैस्ते न्यवृतन्मनोभिः॥१७॥**

**सूतोऽपि गङ्गासलिलैः पवित्वा सहाश्वमात्मानमनल्पमन्युः।  
ससीतयोराघवयोरधीयन् श्वसन् कदुष्णं पुरमाविशेश॥१८॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 14. Better had he died as a flash of lightning or completely withered up like grass, who did not obey his father's order in matters difficult of attainment. What then counts his command to go to forest?

 15. He said, "O citizens return home and remove the sorrow of my father; do not look upon Bharata as other than me." He said to his charioteer, "Turn the chariot.”

 16. Râma, knowing the desire of the people of going with him, departed on the morning under some pretext, after sleeping for one night with them to protect them against the fear of the wild beasts.

 17. The citizens, weeping pitifully, shed tears and over and again sighed heavily. They returned home with their hearts turned to Ráma with the words, "Alas! Ráma."

 18. Even Súta (Ra'ma's charioteer), with his grief intensified, purifying himself with the horses

**प्रतीय सा पूर्ददृशे जनेन द्यौर्भानुशीतांशुनिराकृतेव।  
राजन्यनक्षत्रसमन्वितापि शोकान्धकारक्षतसर्वचेष्टा॥१९॥**

**विलोक्य रामेण विना सुमन्त्रमच्योष्ट सत्वान्नृपतिश्र्युताशः।  
मधूनि नैषीद्व्यलिपन्न गन्धैर्मनोरमे न व्यवसिष्ट वस्त्रे॥२०॥**

**आसिष्ट नैकत्र शुचा व्यरंसीत्  
 कृताकृतेभ्यः क्षितिपालभाग्भ्यः।  
स चन्दनोशीरमृणालदिग्धः  
 शोकाग्निनागाद्युनिवासभूयम्॥२१॥**

**विचुक्रुशुर्भूमिपतेर्महिष्यः केशॉल्लुलुञ्चः स्ववपूंषि जघ्नुः।  
विभूषणान्युन्मुमुचुः क्षमायां पेतुर्बभञ्जुर्वलयानि चैव॥२२॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

into the Gangetic waters, entered the city, remembering Râma, Lakshmaṇa add Sitá and sighing deeply.

 19. The city was seen by the people on their return, like the heaven divested of its sun and moon though (it was) inhabited by the star-like princes,. (still) having all its activities stopped by the darkness of the grief

 20. Seeing Sumantra without Râma the king, quite disappointed (च्युताशः) lost his consciousness, did not desire drinks, did not besmear (his body) with ointments and did not put on beautiful garments.

 21. Through grief the king did not sit at one place, desisted from (attending to) his half-finished regal works; though besmeared with lotus-filaments, Ushira (वाळा) and sandal he went to the state of the gods (i. e. died) on account of his fire-like grief.

 22.The crowned queens of the king wept, tore.

**ताः सान्त्वयन्ती भरतप्रतीक्षा तं बन्धुता न्यक्षिपदाशु तैले।  
दूतांश्च राजात्मजमानिनीषून् प्रास्थापयन्मन्त्रिमतेन यूनः॥२३॥**

**सुप्तो नभस्तः पतितं निरीक्षाञ्चक्रे विवस्वन्तमधः स्फुरन्तम्।  
आख्यद्वमन्मातृकुले सखिभ्यः पश्यन् प्रमादं भरतोऽपि राज्ञः॥२४॥**

**अशिश्रवन्नात्ययिकं तमेत्य दूता यदार्थं प्रयियासयन्तः।  
आंहिष्ट जाताञ्जिहिषस्तदासावुत्कण्ठमानो भरतो गुरुणाम्॥२५॥**

**बन्धूनशङ्किष्ट समाकुलत्वादासेदुषः स्नेहवशादपायम्।  
गोमायुशारङ्गगणाश्च सम्यङ्नायासिषुर्भीममरासिषुश्च॥२३॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

off their hair, struck their bodies, broke off their necklaces; rolled on the ground and destroyed their bracelets.

 23. His kith and kin, comforting the queens and waiting for Bharata's arrival, put him immediately into oil and despatched, with the consent of the councillors, youthful messengers willing to bring him.

 24. Even Bharata, while at his grand-father's, saw, in his sleep, the fall from the sky of the sun shooting downwards; he told this to his friends, — thus foreseeing the calamity to his father.

 25. When the messengers, on their arrival, apprised him of the disastrous event, (thus) creating in him a desire to go, Bharata longing to see his elders and yearning to go, started (on his journey).

 26. Through the flurried state of his mind and through affections Bharata suspected danger to his brothers; (on his way) the jackals and deer did not pass away favourably and screamed terribly.

**स पोषिवानेत्य पुरं प्रवेक्ष्यन्**  
**** **शुश्राव घोषं न जनौघजन्यम्**  
**आकर्णयामास न वेदनादान्न**  
**** **चोपलेभे वणिजां पणायान्॥२७॥**

**चक्रन्दुरुचैर्नृपतिं समेत्य तं मातरोऽभ्यर्णमुपागतास्राः।**  
**पुरोहितामात्यमुखाश्च योधा विवृद्धमन्युप्रतिपूर्णपन्याः॥२८॥**

**दिदृक्षमाणः परितः ससीतं रामं यदा नैक्षत लक्ष्मणं च।**  
**रोरुद्यमानः स तदाभ्यष्टच्छद्यथावदाख्यन्नथ वृत्तमस्मै॥२९॥**

**आबद्धभीमभ्रुकुटीविभङ्गः शेश्वीयमानारुणरौद्रनेत्रः।**  
**उच्चैरुपालब्ध स केकयीं च शोके मुहुश्चाविरतं न्यमाङ्क्षीत्॥३०॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 27. He, a traveller, having entered the city, did not hear the joyous sound of the people on his entry into the house; nor the sound of the recital of the Vedas, and did not see the transaction of commercial business.

 28. The mothers, observing him to approach, lamented for the king very loudly and the army, headed by the family priest and the minister were also overpowered with affliction.

 29. When he, looking on all sides, did not see Ráma with Sita' and Lakshmana, he, with excessive grief, or lamentations, asked (what the matter was); they informed him of the event as it happened.

 30. With the fierce knitting of his eye-brows and his red and terrible eyes swollen he censured Kekayi loudly and plunged himself in repeated and continuous grief.

**नृपात्मजौ चिक्लिशतुः ससीतौ ममार राजा विधवा भवत्यः।  
शोच्या वयं भूरनृपा लघुत्वं केकय्युपज्ञं बत बह्वनर्थम्॥३१॥**

**नैतन्मतं मत्कमिति ब्रुवाणः  
 सहस्रशोऽसौ शपथानशप्यत्।  
उद्वाश्यमानः पितरं सरामं  
 लुठ्यन् सशोको भुवि रोरुदावान्॥३२॥**

**तं सुस्थयन्तः सचिवा नरेन्द्रं दिधक्षयन्तः समुदूहुरारात्।  
अन्याहुतिं हावयितुं सविप्राश्चिचीषयन्तोऽध्वरपात्रजातम्॥३३॥**

**उदक्षिपन् पट्टदुकूलकेतूनवादयन् वेणुमृदङ्गकांस्यम्।  
कम्बूंश्च तारानधमन् समन्तात्तथानयन् कुङ्कुमचन्दनानि ॥३४॥**

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

 31. The two sons of the king, with Sitá, are put to trouble, the king is dead, you are widows, we are to be deplored, the earth is deprived of her king; thus, alas! the self-conceived littleness of the mind of Kekayi is attended by many troubles.

 32. Calling aloud for the king with Râma, full of grief, rolling on the ground and weeping excessively he swore many times that that was not his project.

 33. The ministers, consoling him and inducing him to burn the body of his father, directing (others) to take the sacrificial requisites to give the last oblations, carried the king to a near funeral ground, being accompanied by the priests.

 34. They lifted up banners of silken garments; played on flutes, tabors and cymbals; sounded the conches loudly and took with them saffron and sandal.

**श्रोत्राक्षिनासावदनं सरुक्मं  
 कृत्वाजिने प्राक्शिरसं निधाय।  
सञ्चित्य पात्राणि यथाविधान-  
 मृत्विग्जुहाव ज्वलितं चिताग्निम्॥३५॥**

**कृतेषु पिण्डोदकसञ्चयेषु हित्वाभिषेकं प्रकृतं प्रजाभिः।  
प्रत्यानिनीषुर्विनयेन रामं प्रायादरण्यं भरतः सपौरः॥३६॥**

**शीघ्रायमाणैः ककुभोऽश्नुवानैर्जनैरपन्थानमुपेत्य मृप्तैः।  
शोकादभूपैरपि भूश्चकासाञ्चकार नागेन्द्ररथाश्वमित्रैः॥३७॥**

**उच्चिक्यिरे पुष्पफलं वनानि सस्तुः पितॄन् पिप्रियुरापगासु।  
आरेटुरित्वा पुलिनान्यशङ्कं छायां समाश्रित्य विशश्रमुश्च॥३८॥**

—————————————————————————————————

 35. First placing the head on the antelope hide, putting over the ears, eyes, nose and the mouth pieces of gold and also sacrificial ladles, the priest threw oblations into the lighted funeral fire according to the rites.

 36. After the oblation of obsequies rice-balls and water Bharata, abandoning the coronation by the subjects, went with his citizens to the forest to bring back Râma with humility.

 37. The earth appeared to advantage with the citizens who were hastening, occupying the quarters, who were sleeping on the road, though devoid of ornaments on account of sorrow.

 38. They gathered flowers and fruits of the forest trees; bathed and offered oblations, into the river, to their manes; going (afterwards) to the sandy banks they roamed fearlessly and resorting to a shade took rest.

**सम्प्राप्य तीरं तमसापगाया गङ्गाम्बुसम्पर्कविशुद्धिभाजः।  
विगाहितुं यामुनमम्बु पुण्यं ययुर्निरुद्धश्रमवृत्तयस्ते॥३९॥**

**ईयुर्भरद्वाजमुनोर्निकेतं यस्मिन् विशश्राम समेत्य रामः।  
च्युताशनायः फलवद्विभूत्या व्यस्यन्नुदन्यां शिशिरैः पयोभिः॥४०॥**

**वाचंयमान् स्थण्डिलशायिनश्च युयुक्षमाणाननिशं मुमुक्षून्।  
अध्यापयन्तं विनयात् प्रणेमुः पद्गा भरद्वाजमुनिं सशिष्यम्॥४१॥**

**आतिथ्यमेभ्यः परिनिर्विवप्सोः  
 कल्पद्रुमा योगबलेनं फेलुः।  
धामप्रथिम्नो म्रदिमान्वितानि  
 वासांसि च द्राघिमवन्त्युदुहुः॥४२॥**

**———————————————————————————————**

 39. Reaching the banks of the Tamasâ purified by her confluence with the Gangetic waters they, their fatigues being removed, took their way to sport in the holy waters of the Yamunã.

 40. They reached the hermitage of Bharadvája in which Râma had rested along with his wife and brother feeding himself on the rich abundance of fruit and satisfying his thirst with cool waters.

 41. The pedestriens (पद्नाः) humbly bowed down to the sage Bharadvaja, with his pupils, who was giving instruction to the absolution-wishers who have restrained their speech, who slept on the sacrificial ground and who desired to practise concentration of the wind.

 42. Through the supernatural powers of him who desired to entertain them and who was great by his lustre the trees put forth fruits and very long and soft garments.

**आज्ञां प्रतीषुर्विनयादुपास्थुर्जगुः सरागं ननृतुः सहावम्।  
सविभ्रमं नेमुरुदारमूचुस्तिलोत्तमाद्या वनिताश्च तस्मिन्॥४३॥**

**वस्त्रान्नपानं शयनं च नाना कृत्वावकाशे रुचिसम्प्रक्लृप्तम्।  
तान् प्रीतिमानाह मुनिस्ततः स्म निवध्वमाध्वं पिबतात्तशेध्वम्॥४४॥**

**ते भुक्तवन्तः सुसुखं वसित्वा  
 वासांस्युषित्वा रजनीं प्रभाते।  
द्रुतं समध्वा रथवाजिनागै-  
 र्मन्दाकिनीं रम्यवनां समीयुः॥४५॥**

**वैखानसेभ्यः श्रुतरामवार्तास्ततो विशिञ्जानपतन्त्रिसङ्घम्।  
अभ्रंलिहाग्रं रविमार्गभङ्गमानंहिरेऽद्रिं प्रति चित्रकूटम्॥४६॥**

**———————————————————————————**

 43. There the courtesans, Tillottama' and others, waited for the behests (of the sage). approached with great humility, sang in musical notes, danced with gestures, bent with amorous movements and spoke agreeable and dignified words.

 44. In the interval having prepared dresses, food and drinks, and beds separately, according to the individual taste the kind sage addressed them, “Put on your dresses, take your seats, drink, eat and sleep.”

 45. They, who had taken their meals, put on dresses and slept the night very happily, rising up early in the morning, at once went to the river Mandákinî of beautiful forests, in company and with chariots, horses and elephants.

 46. Thence they, knowing the intelligence of Râma from the hermits, repaired to the mountain Chitrakuta on which birds sweetly warbling were dwelling,

**दृष्ट्वोर्णुवानान् ककुभो बलौघान्  
 वितत्य शार्ङ्गं कवचं पिनह्य।  
तस्थौ सिसङ्ग्रामयिषुः शितेषुः  
 सौमित्रिरक्षिभ्रुवमुज्जिहानः॥४७॥**

**शुक्लोत्तरासङ्गभृतोविशस्तान्  
 पादैः शनैरापततः समन्यून्।  
औहिष्ट तान् वीतविरुद्धबुद्धीन्  
 विवन्दिषून् दाशरथिः स्ववर्ग्यान्॥४८॥**

**समूलकाषं चकषूरुदन्तो-  
 रामान्तिकं बृंहितमन्युवेगाः।  
आवेदयन्तः क्षितिपालमुच्चैः–  
 कारं मृतं रामवियोगशोकात्॥४९॥**

**—————————————————————————**

the summits of which touched the clouds and which obstructed the path of the sun.

 47. Seeing a large army spread over the quarters the son of Sumitrá, stretching his bow and putting on his armour, stood desirous of giving a fight, possessed of deadly arrows and raising up eyes and eye-brows.

 48. The son of Daśaratha inferred them to be his own relations, dressed in white outer garments, devoid of any weapon, slowly walking, full of sorrow and free from unfriendly feelings, wishing to salute.

 49. Approaching Râma they, who were weeping, the force of whose grief was increasing and who were proclaiming the death of the king on account of the grief of Rãma's separation, scratched the earth completely.

**चिरं रुदित्वा करुणं सशब्दं  
 गोत्राभिधायं सरितं समेत्य।  
मध्येजलाद्राघवलक्ष्मणाभ्यां  
 प्रत्तं जलं द्व्यञ्जलसन्तिकेऽपाम्॥५०॥**

**अरण्ययाने सुकरे पिता माम्  
 प्रायुङ्क्त राज्ये बत दुष्करे त्वाम्।  
मागाः शुचं वीर भरं वहामु-  
 माभाषि रामेण वचः कनीयान्॥५१॥**

**कृती श्रुती वृद्धमतेषु धीमां-  
 स्त्वं पैतृकं चेद्वचनं न कुर्याः।  
विच्छिद्यमानेऽपि कुले परस्य  
 पुंसः कथं स्यादिह पुत्रकाम्या॥५२॥**

**अस्माकमुक्तं बहु मन्यसे चे-**

**——————————————————————————**

 50. Weeping piteously and loudly for a long time and (then) coming to the river Gotrã Râma and Lakshmaṇa, descending into the midst of the water, offered each a cavity of water near it.

 51. “Alas! our father has proposed me for an easy journey into the forest, while he has appointed you to conduct the management of the kingdom; do not give yourself up to sorrow, O you warrior, (but) execute the command of our father," thus was addressed his younger brother by Râma.

 52. “If you, who have heard and acted up according to, the precepts of the old, do not abide by your father's speech, will there be desire on the part of another man to have a son though the whole family were destroyed?”

 53. "If you honour our speech, if you do not rule

** द्यदीशिषे त्वं न मायि स्थिते च।  
जिद्व्रेष्यतिष्ठन् यदि तातवाक्ये  
 जहीहि शङ्कां व्रज शाधि पृथ्वीम्॥५३॥**

**वृद्धौरसां राज्यधुरां प्रवोढुं  
 कथं कनीयानहमुत्सहेय।  
मा मां प्रयुक्थाः कुलकीर्तिलोपे  
 प्राह स्म रामं भरतोऽपि धर्म्यम्॥५४॥**

**ऊर्जस्वलं हस्तितुरङ्गमेत-  
 दमूनि रत्नानि च राजभाञ्जि।  
राजन्यकं चैतदहं क्षितीन्द्र-  
 स्त्वयि स्थिते स्यामिति शान्तमेतत्॥५५॥**

**इति निगदितवन्तं राघवस्तं जगाद  
 व्रज भरत गृहीत्वा पादुकेत्वं मदीये।**

**———————————————————————————————**
